¶ About the treatment of a swelling in a broken bone.
This swelling often happens in the treatment of the cure of a broken bone. Especially, which is near the joints: and it becomes unsightly in its figure of the member. And perhaps it forbids the limb from its natural operation.
Therefore consider now, if the swelling is recent: administer to it medicines which have stiptic properties, such as aloe, and olibanum, and myrrh, and sarcocolla, and acacia, and similar of these.
Thus, take some of these, or all of them: and confect a poultice with alcohol, or with albumen, or with vinegar: and put it on the swelling over the wound, and tie it tightly above the good closure.
And let it be loose for days many. Then loose it, and put on another, until the swelling is removed.
Or tie it tightly over the leaden plate. For lead has the property to remove everything that is feared from the limbs.
But if the swelling has already come to the stone, and is tight, and there is a need to remove it quickly: then find over it from its superior part, and cut off the excessive protrusion: or scrape it with some sharp instruments, until it is removed, and take care of it until it is healed.
When treating and restoring the part, and if a small piece remains after that, it is not done according to its nature.
When a bone fracture is restored and a small piece remains after that, weak and brittle, it is not done except for many causes. Of which one is, the multitude of the solution and ligation of its ligaments not according to what is proper: or because of the superfluity of the stricture of the ligaments, until it is forbidden for nourishment to run to the part: or because of the excessive embrocation at an inappropriate time: or because of the scarcity of blood in the weak body and its weakness.
And the treatment of this is to feed the weak and tender body, until blood is multiplied in it. And administer a bath. And introduce joy and merry to him.
Then place a plaster on the part: so that it attracts the plaster to the food much. And he will apply the embrocation of it with hot water, until the nourishment runs and returns to its natural figure.
When bone fragments are restored and bent, it is treated as follows:
When the member is already restored after the healing of the part, and the bent bone or fractured fragment is evident, or nodated: and it is deformed by that form of the bone, nevertheless the part is not prohibited from its natural operation: then it does not need to receive the speech of him who thinks that the member is broken again.
And there have been many foolish physicians and restorers who have done this on our earth.
This indeed is a difficult operation, leading to death or great fear, so that it may be fatal.
However, if the cramp and knot are recent: then it should be rubbed with warm water, in which decoctions of gently-soothing herbs are added, such as the leaves of tall sage, and the root thereof, and melilot, and similar ones. And make for it a plaster with a gently-soothing ointment, such as dyachilon made fittingly.
Or consider mucilage of tall sage, and rub it with chicken fat, or olive oil: and apply it with this.
Or use ripe figs, and bind them with fine linen thread, and similar ones named from decreasing remedies for consolidation.
And sometimes the knot is loosened by persistent rubbing, old, and gentle, which is made with moist hands. And the motion of the limbs is given to all parts in every hour.
However, if the tortuosity has already preceded, and is strong, and has turned to stone, and a need for attending to it with iron has been provoked: then it should be cut off above, and the continuity of bones be loosened, and what remains is that which is superfluous from the knot or bone with sharp saws, and ease is given in it with caution and diligence.
¶ Of Speech in Dislocation.
Dislocation is the exit of some joint from its place. Therefore, movement is prohibited and the appearance of the limb is deformed. And it causes pain to be felt on the infirm part, and severe injuries.
When then a dislocation happens to someone, it is necessary to hurry towards its restoration: nor does it delay it at all.
If it delays, the place will be postponed, and perhaps with it the reduction of the dislocation.
Therefore, it is not necessary to delay, nor to extend in the hour of his postponement. For it often causes a spasm, and painful wounds.
Indeed, when that happens, it is necessary to hurry towards phlebotomy of the weak one. Then he will be released, until the postponement becomes quiet.
Then the member will be rubbed with warm water and oil. Then it will be reduced with ease.
And all the member is to be cared for with that of which the memory will not be in its place.
I myself have already ordered the chapter of dislocation, even according to which the fracture precedes in the body from the upper part to the lower one of it.
¶ About the care of the dislocation of the inferior mandible.
Few dislocations happen to the two mandibles unless rarely.
And the dislocation of the inferior one is made according to one or two modes. Or rather they are removed from their places by a slight removal and are softened somewhat. Or they are dislocated completely and integrally, until they are softened to the chest, until the weak pulse is flowing, and it cannot hold it, nor can it close its mouth, and the tongue of it is uncovered with the speech of it.
When however the dislocation of it is slight, then it returns to several positions by itself with a few number.
If indeed the dislocation is complete and entire, then it must be that a hasty reduction is to be made of its head, and it should not be delayed long: and it is fitting that the physician hold the weak head of the patient: and he inserts his thumb of one hand into the root of the dislocated mandible between the cheeks thereof, if the dislocation is on one side. Or he inserts both of his hands simultaneously, if the dislocation is from two sides. And the rest of his hand should be outside, by which it ought to be equalized.
And he commands the patient to clean his jaws and to free them for chewing to all parts. And the physician should equalize the jaw, and he drives the jaw until it returns to its place.
If however the reduction is difficult, especially if both jaws are at once, then a hasty reduction is to be made of its other side, and he administers ointment with warm water and oil until a easy reduction of the other is made, and it should not be delayed long in reducing the other as we said.
When they are reduced and equalized, and the weak jaw is closed and not softened, then place above them some flax cloths soaked in rose oil, with which a certain thing has been made from wax: and let it be applied.
Then he is to be gently bound with soft bands.
And let the weak one be laid on his back, and his head stand between two flax cloths so that it does not move to the right and left: nor is he compelled or forced to chew anything: indeed, a soft drink of what he desires is placed under his jaw until the pains subside and the jaw nods, then he eats what he wishes and the drink is given with ease.
He should not labor over the operation of his mouth at eating and drinking and nodding until the jaw is subsided and he is cured.
When two mandibles are difficult to reduce after being dislocated in a certain hour and do not return to their original position, many fevers and frequent diarrhea often occur, and perhaps the cold stomach is relieved, and perhaps the sour choler is vomited.
When you see that, then you know that it is a loss, and that person often dies within ten days.
¶ About the reduction of shoulder joint dislocation and its extremity.
Indeed, the shoulder joint is not dislocated from the inner side due to its continuity with the chest. And it is sometimes dislocated to the outside: and this is felt by touch.
And the restoration of it is, that it lie on his weak side on his back, and extend his arms. Then, place it in the right position with the palm of your hand, for it will return.
Then place the bandage over it, and the plasters, and bind it.
But the extremities of these, which follow the elbow, and are continuous with it, are not dislocated, except rarely. When it is dislocated on a certain day: then it must be reduced and made equal according to what I have said, and prepared for you.
Then place the bandage over it, and the plasters, and the bound one. And advise the patient to remain in tranquility and quiet until he recovers.
And when the same treatment is applied to the extremity of the shoulder, it also separates from its place.
¶ About the reduction of shoulder joint dislocation.
You know that the humerus does not dislocate unless it is done in three ways.
One of them is, that it is dislocated to the part that is titillated towards the lower parts. The second way is, that it is dislocated to the part of the chest. And perhaps it is dislocated to the upper part of the humerus: and this happens rarely.
And it is not dislocated to the back, because of the spatula. And it is not dislocated to the front, because of the nerves.
And it is most commonly dislocated and goes towards the lower parts, opposed to the titillated part: especially in those, where the flesh is few. For they go into them easily, and they go in easily.
But in those where the flesh is many, it is opposite to that, because it goes with difficulty, and it goes with difficulty.
And perhaps it happens to some men by hitting, or accident, and the humerus is struck hot, and it is thought because of that, that it is already dislocated.
Therefore, you should try this, until you are certain of it, and then proceed to the treatment of it.
And it is known that dislocation occurs when it is to the lower parts, opposed to the titillated part: and you can distinguish between the dislocated humerus and the sound humerus. For you will find a manifest difference between them, and you will find in the head of the humerus a congestion, and under the titillated part at the touch of the head of the humerus as if it were one thing: and it cannot weakly return the hand to its own ear, nor move it with all movements.
And similarly, if it is dislocated to the front, or upwards: then you will find a manifest touch, and it will not be hidden.
This particular dislocation makes the reduction of this type easier when it is recent or the child is weak.
The reduction of this is that he should raise his helper's hand to the higher parts. Then place your hands under the flap of it, and raise the joint with strength to the top to its own place: and the helper should raise his hand and extend it upwards. Then he should place it lower, for he returns unsteadily himself.
However, if he does not return as we said, and the days that have passed since the dislocation began are many: then it is necessary to let the weak one bathe in hot water, and apply an embrocation that softens and soothes, just as if decoctions of high roots, such as ginger, and fenugreek, and mallow, were applied in water.
Then the weak one should rise on his back: and a sphere and wool that is equal between softness and tightness should be placed under the flap of it.
Then the physician should place his heel on the sphere, and raise the head of the arm with strength, and draw the weak hand downwards.
And if you wish, reduce it according to this manner: and it is that the longer and weaker man should be present, and stand on the side, and insert his arm under the flap of the weaker one: and he should raise the flap of it to the higher parts, until it is weak hanging in the air. And another should draw the weak hand downwards towards his vent. However, if the weak one is large: then it is necessary to hang something else with him, so that it makes it larger, for the dislocation returns immediately.
It is reduced or cut according to another mode: and it is as if a long wooden beam, whose head is round-shaped like the handle of a mortar, were laid on the ground. It should not be thick nor thin. Then soft cloths are placed upon the wooden beam after the other materials have been laid on top of it: and the standing cloth should be as long as the wooden beam.
Then the hand of that person is extended to the lower part of the other side, and the body of that person is extended with strength from that side: for the joint returns to its place with ease.
If it is difficult to reduce the joint through all that I have said, then use this caution: and it should be as if you take a beam whose length is the sum of two forearms, whose width is the sum of four fingers, and whose thickness is the sum of two fingers: and its head should be round like an opening, so that the passage of its head into the deep end can be made according to this form.
Then soft cloths are tied around the round head: so that it does not hurt the weak standing cloth.
Then the weak standing cloth is placed under the opening, so that the hand or forearm can be extended up to the lower part of the beam: and the beam is laid on the aid, and the hand and forearm.
Then the forearm is placed on the step of the ladder across its breadth, and the hand is extended downwards, and the rest of the body is suspended from the other side. For the joint immediately enters.
When the passage of the joint is completed according to any possible mode: then it is necessary to place a round ball of equal quantity from wool under the opening. Then place the prepared plaster made from flour with oil and almond, and spread it all over the shoulder as it rotates.
Then tighten the knot under the thin strip in a neat manner: and roll up the ligament over the plaster: and attach her hand to the neck of its own collar, and let it go, and do not make her hand move for seven days.
And it is necessary that a weak food be placed on him, until he recovers, because it is more beneficial for his cure.
Then a larger one will be placed on him after seven days, or five, and it will be moved with movement. If then he holds firm and is not softened, he is already cured.
If however the joint is dislocated many times, due to the moisture that happens to him, or due to another cause: then it is necessary that this cauterization be given to him with three holes, according to what was said in the chapter on cauterization.
If however this is done all, and the ligament is unwound after seven days, and the joint is not confirmed, and the plaster and the knot are reapplied on it several times, and it does not hold firm, and falls away, and cannot be raised up to the upper part: then you know that the nerves of his arm which are towards the head of the shoulder are already cut, or extended, or softened: and you know that the joint is never set in its place.
However, dislocations of the chest and breast, or to a posterior reduction, are made with a push and extension with the hands: until it returns, and the remaining stricture and care are given with the cauterization, until it is cured.
If however it happens after his cure, old age in the member, and slowness in its movement, then a weak bath is used many times, until that old age is eased, and it returns to its original nature.
Translation: "On the Treatment of the Dislocation of the Knee-joint.
Translation: "You know that a dislocation of the knee-joint is difficult to reduce and also returns with difficulty, and it itself is reduced to all parts, especially to the anterior and posterior.
Translation: "And its dislocation is not hidden from you: for it is a falling under the touch, and under the touch, according to whichever figure it is dislocated. Translation: "And when you join a dislocated knee-joint with its proper bone, this is clearly shown to you. Translation: "The joint is contracted, and it cannot double the arm, nor touch its own shoulder with it.
Translation: "And it is necessary to straighten it as soon as possible, and to reduce the dislocation before a hot application causes it.
Translation: "For if a hot application is given, it becomes difficult to reduce the knee-joint: and perhaps it cannot be cured at all, especially when it is towards the back. Translation: "For it is the worst of all kinds of dislocations, and the most severe pain that comes with them: and often it ends with death.
Translation: "And the restoration of it is, when it can be reduced, to extend the hand of the patient with two hands, and the arm of the patient be expanded, and the hand of the physician to be above the knee and under it, and he himself presses the joint with his thumbs of both hands at once, or with the root of his finger, until it returns to its place.
Translation: "Or if the dislocation is to the front, then it returns thus, that the hand doubles with its own hand, until it strikes with the root of the finger's palm of the dislocated knee-joint.
Translation: "But if the dislocation does not yield to reduction, then administer a violent extension, with strong support: and it is that the two assistants extend the arm, and hold it even, so that it does not remove from the extension.
Then the arm is rotated around until it has been fully unfolded over the palm of the same hand, either the wide spread or the wide ligament.
And when the physician touches it with his own hand, he should grease it with oil: so that this may be one of those which aid in the lubrication of the joint with ease. Then he will drive the joint with a strong push, until it returns.
And after the reduction, it should be placed on the same plaster, where the stipes and excoriation are, with the gouge: and it should be tied in a proper manner. And the arm is hung from the weak neck: and let it hang for some days.
Then it is loosened. If the joint is firm in its place, then loosen the ligament from it and let it go.
And if you see the joint not well tied, then repeat the plaster, and the ligament, and let it go for some days until it is tied. Then let it go.
But if, however, stiffness occurs after the reduction of the joint, and the position is in motion: then administer hydration in the bath, and subtle friction, and compression, until it becomes soft, or place it on the joint itself, that is, the pale heel of the mare. Then it is tied, and let it go on it for a day and night. Then take it away, and introduce it into the bath. When it is wet, then friction the joint with subtle friction. Then repeat on it, with one drop of ointment once, and twice, and thrice with the introduction of the bath, until it becomes soft.
If you wish, place a damp cloth soaked in warm butter over the excrement and then tightly wrap it.
Do this several times. Indeed, it becomes soft again and returns to its original position.
The treatment of the dislocated Almahassem:
The Almahassem hand is often dislocated, that is, the joint between the shoulder and the wrist.
And the reduction of this dislocation is easy. And the opposite joints are reduced. However, it is necessary that the ease of reduction is in the reduction of this dislocation at the time it is dislocated, before the wound is closed, or else a hot apostemetic will occur.
And the reduction of the dislocation is such that you place the Almahassem hand of the patient on a board, and extend the other hand of his: and the physician places his palm on the prominence of the joint, and pushes it, until it returns.
However, it is necessary that it be considered if the palm has already retreated to the vent of the hand: then the physician places the weak back of his hand on the board at the extension and reduction: and if the dislocation is proceeding to the back of the hand, then the position of his hand intrinsically on the board is such that the hand of the physician falls on the same prominence of the joint.
If it returns immediately, that is good: and if not, then tightly wrap it with a sedative wound dressing: and release it, do not insist on this.
For if it does not bear, nor can you stand upon the reduction of it after three days have passed, yet the joint remains according to its twist: and nothing will hurt the weak one, unless the hand has been softened and cannot be tightened around anything: then you know that the nerves are cut or torn.
It is not genius in her, unless it resonates with the cauterization near the site of her wound. And perhaps she will not yield to this.
When Almahassem does not return, then place the ointment over it, which we have mentioned. Then it is bound, and it is released five days later: then it is dissolved and it is released, and the hand is exercised.
If however, the movement of her is hindered, and something of age happens to it: then soothe it with warm water and repeated rubbing, until it is soothed.
¶ Regarding the treatment of the dislocation of the fingers.
When a finger is dislocated from one of them to all parts, or from one of them to the back of the palm, or to its ventrum: then extend the finger, and press the dislocation with your thumbs, until it returns.
Then support the head of the finger, and suspend the opposite part to which it is dislocated, and release it after two days.
Then suspend it, and extend it until it is straight in that day.
When it is night, tie it according to the description: and do not cease to untie it in the day: and exercise it with motion: and tie it at night.
Do this for a few days until it is bound.
And do the same with that, if it is dislocated to the ventrum of the hand: and tie it to the same part, and do the first operation of your hand until it is cured.
And do the same with that when it is dislocated to other parts:
¶ Regarding the treatment of the dislocation of the vertebrae of the back.
When a spondylalgia occurs on the back or a complete dislocation of the neck, or when many vertebrae are moved from their places: then there is no cure for it, because death comes to the weak one.
And the sign of this is that the weak ones leave it without will, and cannot hold it: and often some limbs of him are made soft by it, or two thighs of him, or two arms of him, or one of them: or a vertebra is removed from its place.
For it is often removed, because it is often removed to four parts.
And that which is removed to the back, is called gibbosity.
But the cure of it is, that you consider now, if gibbosity happens from childhood: then there is no cure for it, nor any healing at all from it.
But in those which happen by chance, or by blow, or by such like things, the ancients have already spoken of the ways of curing them with a long discourse. Whose most does not return with profit.
I have already abbreviated from that, whose little excuses from much of its nearness to my proximity: and from my own explanation, in which I explained it, and shaped the instrument of it in a different way from that which they showed it, and they also explained it.
I say therefore, that in gibbosity which happens from the front part, there is no genius, nor healing from it.
And also in those which are made to two sides, even so.
And it is not cured from them, unless those which happen in the back properly, when I say: and it is, that the weak one extends over his face over the podium equal to the wall, and is extended under it with a wet string, so that his heart is not harmed.
Then a straight log is placed on the fire on the ground, at the tip of its foot, roughly at its head: and another log, roughly at its other foot: and it is held by a servant, and it should not be fixed in the fire. And the other holds the other.
Then it is wrapped over the weak head, and the rope of its titillum is gently made firm, and extended from the end of the rope to the log that is at its head: and it is tied in it.
Then string another rope above its belly, and above its own knees, and at its knee joints. Then gather all the ropes together and tie them in the log that is at its foot.
Then the servant extends all of the ropes to a part of the log, and the two logs are not removed from their places where they were fixed: but they should not be made firm as I said.
And the physician will place his hands over the spine with strength, until he returns: or he may place them over the table. Then he will tie the table to his feet, until he returns.
If he does not return through this treatment, then take the table whose length is roughly three arms. And make an opening in the wall near where we said it was weak, in the place where it enters the end of the table. Then place the middle of the table over the spine. And the physician will place his feet over the opposite end. And tie it well, until the spine is bent, and return to its own place.
And if you wish, operate with the turn that was wrapped with your hand: and it is as if you were drawing two logs on the ground at the weak head of the other foot's last toe, and at its head two logs with the length of an arm.
The length of the one palm tree between all timber is and already made in all places a hole, through which the turmoil runs: and the whole wood is firmly fixed on earth much, so that it does not move at all.
The round wood is inserted and there is turmoil in which the ligament rotates in two holes of two timber.
At the end of it there is a hole, in which the wood is fixed, whose length is palm with that of which it rotates: and in two other timber is similar to this.
Then strengthen the ligaments that you have tied in the weak timber which is at the head of it: and the ligaments that you have tied in the thighs of it in the timber which is at the foot of it.
Then stand at all timber which rotates the hand of him with his sinew, with which it rotates the turmoil: and the physician should adjust the spine, according to what we said.
This is the form of the timber which follows.
Then after the spine has returned and adjusted the place, then it is necessary to put the plaster of egg white with almond on it. Then the stupe. Then put above the plaster a star from the tablet, whose width is three digits, or about them. And the length of it has quantity which occupies the place of Gibbositatis, and over a part of the healthy spine.
Then it is placed with the ligament that we said. And it uses weak nutrition of fine, until it becomes healthy.
What if there remains some prominence in the place at the end of the cure: then it is necessary to give medicines which soften and relax, with the administration of the tablet which we said for a long time. The lead sheet is now being applied in that place.
When an exuberance occurs at the posterior spine of the vertebra, it is supposed to be a dislocation, and that is the bone that is now protruding. Therefore, it does not suit to make a presumption of that in this medicine, perhaps it will cause death to happen.
¶ About the treatment of Anchaea's dislocated bone.
You know that in the joints of Anchaea and the shoulder, a dislocation only occurs, and it does not happen to them that happens in other joints due to removal of a small part and union.
And the joint of Anchaea is indeed dislocated according to four parts. That is, because it is dislocated towards the front and back.
And it is indeed most dislocated towards the inside, and little dislocated towards the front and back.
And the treatment of the dislocation towards the inside is: because when you join the weak thigh bone with a sound one with a weak one, it is longer: and the knee of his is more prominent than the sound one, and the weak one cannot extend its foot at the groin: and it is the place that follows the groin, which is burned after being struck by the post, therefore, because the head of the hip has already become wet there.
And the sign that happens to the outside is what are its opposite symptoms, which are the opposite symptoms of these.
And the sign that happens to the front is because the sound thigh expands according to the complement, verily it does not extend itself without pain in the knee: and if it attempts to walk, it cannot go over that to the front: and it retains its urine, and it is struck at the groin of its, and there is a callosity of its foot under the heel during walking.
The sign of this which is subject to dislocation is towards the posterior end, so that it does not extend the knee, nor can it double itself before doubling the thigh: and the leg of its other side is shorter: and the thigh is softened, and the head of the hip is at the place of that one.
However, reduction of this dislocation is as follows, so consider this now if the dislocation is old and has been long in place. Then if you perform a reduction of this, and it remains according to its position: then there is no cure at all in it. Therefore, it does not follow that it should be presumed to be completely healed.
However, in the one where the dislocation is recent and it is of one of the four kinds of dislocations: then you should hurry to this, so that you want to join it, and extend it both towards the interior and towards the exterior, and move it to the right and left: perhaps it will return, and there will be no need for further treatment.
However, if it does not return in this way, then it is necessary to prepare a strong attendant and extend the leg of its other side: or with his hands, or with the ligature that is placed on the leg of its above the knee: and another attendant extends his hand over the tendon of its, so that he inserts his hands under the tendon of its. Then he should tie it with a soft ligature over the root of the hip, and hold the other end of the ligature.
And it should be extension either from the front part of the thigh to the shoulder, or from the back to the back.
And it should be extension of all of them in one way, until the weak one hangs down with its body from the earth, and remains hanging.
And this is the sign of the species of extension, which is common to the four kinds of dislocations.
If then the dislocation returns with that which I said, it is good: and if not, then necessary is all kind of that which I say about the cure proper.
In reduction of this proper when the dislocation is to the inside, it is necessary that the weak be raised above its own sound side. Then he will put a ligament over the head of the hip, in which is the head of the hip and the place which is under the thigh. Then extend the ligament from the part of the thigh up to the higher part of the body, to the knee.
Then he will take another helper to the strong arm of his, and he will make the weak place of the poor hip less, and he will extend it with a strong extension. Now then it returns to its own place.
And this species is easier than the others in the cure of this member.
If however he is excused from you, and does not obey to the coming with this kind of cure altogether: then it is necessary that two weak feet be tied together with a strong and soft ligament over two heels, and over two knees: and the length of each should be according to the quantity of four digits of his own: and the weak knee should be extended more than the other quantity of two digits.
Then he will hang the weak over the head of the wooden beam which is in the house: and the length of it should be according to the quantity of two arms.
Then let the strong ivy cover the head of the hip: and he will hang with the weak in another place: and he will press the strong one to cover the hip with strength, for the dislocation returns to its own place with feebleness.
In the reduction of this proper when the dislocation is to the outside, it is necessary that the weak lie on the foot according to what we have said about the hunchback. The ligament is tied above his thigh, the weakest part properly, and above his chest.
Then two beams are placed, one at his foot, and another at the head of the same. Then a beam is placed in the middle of his foot, fixed well: above which the wet bands are wrapped, so as not to harm the weak one; and it should be a beam between his thighs, so as not to attract the lower parts during extension.
Then all the assistants extend to their own part, and the physician makes the reduction with his own hand.
If then he obeys for reunion, it will be good.
And if not, place over the same tablet, and press it down evenly, as we said in the swelling. Nevertheless, it is necessary that the weak one lie on his sound side.
And when the dislocation is to the front, then it is necessary that the weak leg be extended quickly: and this is according to the same narration over the foot: and the physician places his right hand palm on the weak thigh. Then he bends it with one hand and places the extended leg down to the lower part of the knee.
And if the dislocation is to the back: then it is not necessary that the weak leg be extended downwards and be raised on the ground, but it should be placed on a hard surface, as it is necessary that it also be placed if the dislocation is far away from his exterior, according to what we said about his pillow over two anchas: and it should be on his face and the extended ligaments: according to what we said earlier.
And it is necessary that compression be given with the table also over the place where he is going to be united, when the reduction is complete according to what is appropriate.
When the sign of its ransom is revealed to you, and it is that you extend two weak legs: and when you see them equal, and the weak one tightens its own thigh, and expands it without exception: then you know that the limb has returned, according to what is appropriate: then join the two buttocks, and put on plaster, and tie with a rope, as if it were a wheel: so that it does not swell to any part: and it will hold firmly by quiet for three or four days.
Then solve the ligament, and the plaster, and compare the thigh with the other: then if you see them equal in state, then you know that the dislocation is already fixed: therefore, loosen the weak one for walking.
And if you see something of softening: then return, and that plaster, and tie it according to your first stricture: and release it also for three days.
Then solve that and make it slow in walking over both for a few days, until it is well strengthened.
¶ Regarding the dislocation of the knee.
The knee is dislocated in three ways. The knee is dislocated to the outside, to the inside, and to the lower part, that is, to the back.
And it is not dislocated to the front.
The sign of its dislocation is that it extends the weak leg, so that it applies its thigh to its own buttock. However, if it does not hold with the buttock: then you know that the knee is dislocated.
And the restoration of all modes of its dislocation is that you make the weak leg sit straight, so that it can extend its thigh if possible over there, and hold your back to its face: and place its foot between your feet.
Then hold on with your thumbs to its knee, and make straight lines over its knee with your fingers. Then apply with your hand's side two sides of its knee with strength. And another one extends its foot, until it returns its knee to its place.
And the sign of return is that it holds on with the knee to the thigh without labor.
Then apply the plaster itself, and hold on the knee with the thigh. Then bind it together for three or four days. Then release it.
And do not use it for weak walking for a few days until it heals.
However, if it refuses your return, then administer a strong extension with the bands that precede the report in the care of Anchaea, until it returns.
¶ Treatment of Calcaneal Dislocation.
The heel sometimes comes off by gentle removal and sometimes it is dislocated according to the complement. And its dislocation is made, either to the inside or outside.
And the sign of its dislocation is that you see the heel inflated, going to the part where it is dislocated.
However, the treatment of its removal is a easy return: and it is that it extends with ease with the hands and becomes equal until it returns.
In the treatment of it when it is dislocated according to the complement, it is necessary that you make the weak sit upright: and hold it with a strong hand from the back part of its back, in the middle of it.
Then hold on with your right hand's foot of its part from the top of it and with your left hand's foot from the bottom part of its foot's place.
Then pull your foot towards you with your right hand. Then with your left hand, make two incisions.
Then extend with your left hand, and push the chest of the foot with your right leg without effort.
Do this twice, as we have described.
Then push the chest of the foot towards the third thigh, and you will pull out the catheter.
If then it returns to the third thigh, or to two in this manner, and you see the foot equalized, it is good: and if not, then repeat the operation on this one, because it returns itself.
If however, it is forbidden for you to return it with what we have described: then make the weak side lie on its back over the ground, and fasten a bandage on the firm ground, so that it falls between its thighs: and you will have already covered it with the cloth, so that it does not hurt the weak one.
Then let the attendant take the thigh of his patient: then extend the other foot, either with his hands, or with the bandage that lies on the neck of the foot. Then let the attendant extend all against the comparison of his extension: and let the bandage stand between the two weak thighs, holding it, so that it does not attract the body of its towards the lower parts in the extension.
Then the physician should straighten the dislocation with his hands, and the other attendant should hold the sound thigh down to the lower parts. For the dislocation returns with weakness.
When then the dislocation returns, and it declares to you the health of its return: then put on the plaster and ointment, and tie it firmly with the strong bandages, and tie the foot with the bandage to the thigh.
And it is necessary that you should be careful or fear that the nerves which are above the heel posterior may not be hurt by the bandage, and it does not become a burden to them.
Then release it for two days, or three. If the ligament is softened, tighten it again: then dissolve it on the third day, or the fourth: and forbid the weak one from walking for forty days. If however he dares to walk before that time, there is no security that it will not be dissolved by the displacement over it, and it will rot, and will not receive after that treatment.
If however a hot aposteme occurs on him: then it is necessary to administer to him in the sedative of his this, which precedes our narrative in another place from our cure, and embrocation, until it be removed.
¶ About the displacement of the toes of the foot, and the cure.
It is necessary that it be equal to what is dislocated from them with a small extension without labor: and this in fact is not difficult, but rather easy.
If however the displacement is in some of the nodes of the back of the foot: then it is necessary that he make sit the weak leg of his on a place equal to the earth, or on a table: and stand as if walking.
Then you stand, and place your foot on that which is described from those joints. Then shoe it with your foot with virtue, until it returns, and you will see that it is equal, and will not appear in the place of eminence.
Then place under the belly of his foot a table which can hold the whole foot, with two handles. Then tighten it for three days according to the curve. Then dissolve it, and make him rest from walking for many days, until it be tightened and secured from the reduction.
¶ About the kinds of displacement with a sinew or fracture.
When something happens from this, and the care of it and its restoration follows, death often follows: and therefore it is not fitting that they assume responsibility for the care of this kind of thing without being very experienced in the art, merciful, kind, slow, not bold, nor presumptuous: and to use in medicine when it stands still, apostemata hot only: and let go the weak for a long time. Unless you see, or hope for health from the destruction with the ease of the disease, and in which something seems to be hoped for. Therefore, let them immediately perform the reduction of this, before the hot apostemata occur.
If then the member returns according to our will, at that time we should administer the regimen which soothes the hot apostemata: and care for the opposite side with it that agrees with him from desiccated ointments.
If then dislocation is with fracture, and happen in the separated bones of the saturation, at that time let them dare to remove it, and make in that what I said in simple diseases from that which precedes in those places.
And take care according to your virtue, and guard yourself from the fear within until it comes to my prediction for you.
For this is better for you before your Creator, and more lasting to your glory, and more safe for your body among them in your time.
